data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Two%20dimensional%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Two dimensional materials</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode+Materials&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode%20materials">anode materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Vmos2&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_VMoS2">VMoS<sub>2</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Defects&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_defects">defects</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Layered+Structure&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_layered%20structure">layered structure</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Voltage+Range&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_voltage%20range">voltage range</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Recently, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been widely considered to be the possible alternatives of lithium-ion batteries in the field of energy storage owing to the rich sodium resource in the world. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 1 to 6">(1−6)</a> However, compared with Li<sup>+</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup> with a larger radius is not conducive to the intercalation between graphite layers of commercial graphite-based anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 7 and 8">(7,8)</a> To date, developing suitable anode materials with excellent electrochemical performance for the SIBs is highly challenging. Carbon material-, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> metal oxide-, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> metal sulfide-, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> and alloy <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a>-based anodes have been developed and applied in the SIBs. Typically, MoS<sub>2</sub>, a kind of layered metal sulfide, has drawn numerous attention since it has larger interlayer spacing as well as higher theoretical capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 13 to 15">(13−15)</a> Unfortunately, the pure MoS<sub>2</sub> has poor electrical conductivity and slow reaction kinetics and shows a large volume change in the process of intercalation/deintercalation, resulting in a short cycling life and low-rate capacity, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16,17)</a> which need to be overcome.</div><div class="NLM_p">To solve the above issues, various strategies such as designing of nanostructures, electrolyte selection, cutting off voltage range, and searching for electrode materials have been adopted. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 18 to 25">(18−25)</a> By using these strategies, increased Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites, shortened pathway for Na<sup>+</sup> transmission, enhanced conductivity, and/or mitigated volume expansion could be realized. For instance, Ye et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> synthesized a MoS<sub>2</sub> anode material with mixed phases for SIBs, which showed specific capacities of 200 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> and 154 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> in the 0.4–3 V voltage range. Even though it displayed a good cycling stability, the reversible capacity was still low so that it could not meet the demand of application. Wang et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> fabricated a C@MoS<sub>2</sub>@polypyrrole anode material, which showed a remarkable specific capacity of 713 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> and an excellent cyclability with a specific capacity of 295 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup> in 500 cycles. However, the fabrication process was too complex; hence, this anode was not suitable for commercial SIBs. Therefore, the fabrication of suitable anode materials with a remarkable property for SIBs via facile methods is still necessary.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, a novel orderly layered VMoS<sub>2</sub> (OL-VMS) material was synthesized through a simple self-assembly hydrothermal approach followed by a calcination treatment process. This material had an interesting nanostructure with stacked nanosheets, which could offer abundant storage sites for Na<sup>+</sup>, vertical paths to promote the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, and a stable framework to mitigate the volume change during the intercalation/deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup>. As it was used as the anode material, a remarkable specific capacity of 602.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was obtained at 0.200 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Furthermore, after 190 cycles at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the OL-VMS-based anode still exhibited an outstanding specific capacity of 534.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. In addition, under the condition of large current density of 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, a specific capacity of 452.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was achieved.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Synthesis of OL-VMS</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The OL-VMS material was simply synthesized as follows. First, NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub> (0.234 g) and Na<sub>2</sub>MoO<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O (0.3629 g) were mixed in distilled water (30 mL) with ammonia solution (0.25 mL) by ultrasonic treatment. Second, C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>5</sub>NS (TAA, 2.4 g) was mixed with the above solution and vigorously stirred for 30 min. Then, the obtained clear suspension was transferred into a Teflon-lined sealed autoclave (50 mL), which was placed in an oven (220 °C) for 24 h. Thereafter, the autoclave was cooled down to ambient temperature, and the black product was recovered by centrifugation and completely washed with distilled water and ethanol. Then, the product was dried in a vacuum oven (60 °C) for 12 h, followed by calcination at 300 °C for 1 h in an argon environment. For comparison, the OL-VMS materials synthesized with different amounts of ammonia solution and reaction periods were also obtained using the same process. The characterizations of the obtained materials and electrochemical measurements are described in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">To explore the formation of OL-VMS with the special structure, the synthesis of OL-VMS with different amounts of ammonia solutions was carried out. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a> shows the SEM images of different OL-VMS samples prepared with different amounts of ammonia solutions, i.e., 0, 0.5, 1, and 2 mL. In the absence of ammonia solution, a flower-like morphology was obtained (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>a,b). When 0.25 mL of ammonia solution was added, the orderly layered arrangement structure formed by stacked nanosheets with a diameter of 200 nm was clearly observed in 24 h (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a,b). With an increase in the amount of ammonia solution, the morphology of the orderly layered structure became more and more nonuniform (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>c–h). For example, as the added ammonia solution was increased to 1 and 2 mL, the nanosheets with a larger diameter of ∼500 nm coexisted with those nanosheets with a diameter of 200 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>e,g). Thus, a controllable addition of an appropriate amount of ammonia solution should be conducive to the formation of more orderly layered structures.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/medium/am1c05096_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/large/am1c05096_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a,b) SEM images of OL-VMS. (c) EDS elemental mapping of S, Mo, and V. (d) TEM and (e,f) HRTEM images of OL-VMS.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/large/am1c05096_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c05096&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a> shows the SEM images of OL-VMS samples synthesized with different hydrothermal synthesis time periods. One can see that the orderly layered structure with a short length was formed in the first 3 h (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>a,b). In addition, the single-layer nanosheet was also observed in this stage (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>b), indicating that the single-layer nanosheet should be first formed at the beginning stage of the reaction. Then, with an increase in the hydrothermal synthesis time from 3 to 18 h, the length of the pillar with the orderly layered structure increased gradually (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>c–h). In the following sections, the OL-VMS prepared with 0.25 mL of NH<sub>4</sub>OH and 24 h reaction will be mainly characterized and discussed since it showed the best performance in the preliminary experiments. Even though the synthesis time was increased to 36 h (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>), the morphology was almost the same as that of the sample prepared with a time of 24 h. Based on these SEM observations, the formation mechanism of OL-VMS with the orderly layered  structure is illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>. Herein, because of the lowest energy of the (002) plane of the MoS<sub>2</sub> crystal, the MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheet could be formed along the (002) plane during the hydrothermal process at first. Then, the NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> from NH<sub>4</sub>OH in solution would adsorb on the surface of the formed nanosheet so that the nanosheet was positively charged. As such, MoO<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup> and VO<sub>3</sub><sup>–</sup> anions in the solution were also adsorbed on the positively charged nanosheet surface through electrostatic interactions. Thereafter, the anions could be reduced by TAA to form the other VMS nanosheet, which would be stacked together with the first VMoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheet. This nanosheet stacking process should be proceeded continuously during the hydrothermal synthesis as the reaction time increases. Finally, the orderly layered structure was obtained, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a,b. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c shows the EDS elemental mapping of various elements on the surface of the obtained OL-VMS sample. Obviously, the elements Mo, S, and V were uniformly distributed. According to the EDS analysis result shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>, the elemental ratio of V and Mo was around 1.63.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/medium/am1c05096_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/large/am1c05096_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Schematic illustration of the OL-VMS fabrication procedure.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/large/am1c05096_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c05096&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The nanostructure of OL-VMS was further observed by TEM measurements. From <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d, it was obviously seen that the orderly layered structure with a diameter of 200 nm was consistent with the SEM observation (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a,b). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e,f presents HRTEM images at different parts. It was clearly seen that a smaller interlayer spacing of ∼0.6 nm was observed when compared with that of pure MoS<sub>2</sub> (0.615 nm). The smaller interlayer spacing in the OL-VMS could have been resulted from the replacement of the Mo atom in the framework of MoS<sub>2</sub> by the V atom. In addition, after the addition of V species, the (002) plane of MoS<sub>2</sub> became chaotic and discontinuous, resulting in more defects in the OL-VMS sample (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e,f), which could offer abundant Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a displays the typical XRD spectra of the obtained OL-VMS samples, in which four main peaks located at 2θ = 14.56°, 33.07°, 39.51°, and 58.29° are attributed to the (002), (100), (103), and (110) planes of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS no. 37-1492), respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> It should be noted that no peaks relating to the impurities were found, indicating that the pure-phase OL-VMS was obtained. Moreover, the position of the peak corresponding to the (002) plane was just located in between the (002) plane peak of MoS<sub>2</sub> and the (001) plane peak of VS<sub>2</sub>, revealing that the interlayer spacing of OL-VMS should be larger than that of VS<sub>2</sub> but smaller than that of MoS<sub>2</sub>. In addition, based on Bragg’s law, the calculated distance between the interlayers was 0.6 nm, which was consistent with the HRTEM observation (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e,f). Notably, the characteristic peaks of VS<sub>2</sub> were not detected from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a, indicating the formation of a new phase because of the incorporation of vanadium into the nanostructure of MoS<sub>2</sub>. As indicated above, it caused smaller interlayer spacing. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a> shows the XRD spectra of those OL-VMS samples prepared under different conditions. One can see that all XRD spectra were similar, revealing that they had the same crystalline structure. To further verify the nanostructure of the OL-VMS sample, Raman spectrum analysis was also performed. Since the Raman spectrum of MoS<sub>2</sub> is almost the same as that of VS<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref30 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 29 to 31">(29−31)</a> as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b, three peaks appeared at ∼290, 388, and 410 cm<sup>–1</sup>, which corresponded to E<sub>1g</sub>, <span class="NLM_inline-formula"><i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer inline-formula-styles" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/medium/am1c05096_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i6"></span>, and A<sub>1g</sub> modes of MoS<sub>2</sub> as well as VS<sub>2</sub>, respectively. In particular, the peak observed at ∼150 cm<sup>–1</sup> indicated the presence of V–S bonds in the framework of MoS<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32,33)</a> which was consistent with the above XRD result. Moreover, since the previous works have confirmed that the positions of the 1T phase of MoS<sub>2</sub> and VS<sub>2</sub> in the Raman spectrum were similar, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref34" aria-label="reference 29 and 34">(29,34)</a> the 1T and 2H phases should coexist in the structure of OL-VMS. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a> displays the XPS survey spectrum and high-resolution XPS spectra of Mo 3d, V 2p, and S 2p for the OL-VMS. The peaks corresponding to V, Mo, and S were clearly observed (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a), indicating the coexistence of S, Mo, and V species in the framework of the obtained OL-VMS sample. In the high-resolution XPS spectrum of Mo 3d (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b), four peaks were observed. Among them, two strong peaks located at 228.4 and 231.5 eV were ascribed to Mo<sup>4+</sup> 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Mo<sup>4+</sup> 3d<sub>3/2</sub>, respectively, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> whereas the small peaks appearing at 235.8 and 225.9 eV were assigned to Mo<sup>6+</sup> and S 2s, respectively. Herein, the presence of Mo<sup>6+</sup> could be due to the oxidation process on the surface of MoS<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> For V 2p, four peaks at 513.3, 521.0, 517.5, and 524.8eV corresponding to V<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, V<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, V<sup>4+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, and V<sup>4+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively, were observed (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c). Herein, the formation of V<sup>2+</sup> could be resulted from the strong reducibility of S<sup>2–</sup>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> which was produced from the decomposition of TAA at high temperature. The high-resolution XPS spectrum of S 2p consisted of four peaks located at 164.1, 162.8, 161.7, and 161.0 eV, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d), in which the two peaks in the low binding energy were attributed to S<sup>2–</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and S<sup>2–</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> However, in the high binding energy, there were two peaks corresponding to S<sup>0</sup> and <span class="NLM_inline-formula"><i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer inline-formula-styles" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/medium/am1c05096_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i7"></span>, which could be produced by the decomposition of excessive TAA in the process of synthesis. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 39 and 40">(39,40)</a> Combining these XPS analysis results with the above HRTEM, XRD, and Raman analyses, it was observed that some parts of molybdenum in the nanostructure of MoS<sub>2</sub> should be replaced by vanadium, leading to the formation of VMoS<sub>2</sub>.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/medium/am1c05096_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/large/am1c05096_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) XRD pattern and (b) Raman spectrum of the OL-VMS sample.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/large/am1c05096_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c05096&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/medium/am1c05096_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/large/am1c05096_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) XPS survey spectrum of OL-VMS. High-resolution (b) Mo 3d, (c) V 2p, and (d) S 2p spectra of OL-VMS.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/large/am1c05096_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c05096&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The CVs of the OL-VMS-based anodes were collected under the condition of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> within a voltage range of 0.1–3 V. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a displays the first three cycles of CVs for the OL-VMS-based anode, in which four cathodic peaks at 2.3, 1.9, 1.37, and 0.3 V as well as two anodic peaks at 2.22 and 1.55 V appeared. Herein, the cathodic peaks at 2.3, 1.9, and 1.37 V could be assigned to the intercalation process between the interlayers, while the last peak at 0.3 V indicated the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>S with metal Mo during the conversion process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> Herein, the intensity of the cathodic peak located at 0.3 V disappeared, and the CV curve remained almost unchanged after the first cycle (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a), which could be assigned to a stable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer produced by the ether-based electrolyte after the first cycle. Meanwhile, the peaks at 1.55 and 2.22 V in the first anodic scan should be assigned to either the formation of MoS<sub>2</sub> during the conversion process or the deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> from the interlayers.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/medium/am1c05096_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/large/am1c05096_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Typical CV curves of the OL-VMS-based anode in the first three cycles under the condition of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> within a voltage range from 0.1 to 3 V. (b) Typical galvanostatic charging–discharging curves in the first, second, and fifth cycles for the OL-VMS-based anode at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Stability measurement of the OL-VMS-based anode at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Rate capability of the OL-VMS-based anode. (e) Long cyclability of the OL-VMS-based electrode at 5A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/large/am1c05096_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c05096&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b shows the galvanostatic charging–discharging curves of the first, second, and fifth cycles for the OL-VMS-based anode, which were tested at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> within a voltage ranging from 0.1 to 3 V. Herein, high specific capacities of 791.8 and 646.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for the first cycle with a high initial Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 81.6% were obtained. The irreversible capacity in the initial cycle was ascribed to the formation of an SEI later. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 42 and 43">(42,43)</a> Subsequently, after five cycles, the specific capacity was 602.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a CE of ∼100%. Herein, the electrolyte with ether solvent could produce a stable SEI layer on the electrode surface, leading to a high initial CE. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> The long cycling stability at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> was measured. From <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c, it was obviously seen that the anode delivered a stable specific capacity of 534.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> until 190 cycles. Furthermore, as another evaluation standard, the rate performance of the OL-VMS-based anode was also measured. As displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d, the OL-VMS-based anode exhibited reversible capacities of 601.4, 586.2, 564.2, 527.7, and 452.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Furthermore, it was worth noting that the reversible capacity was rebounded to 550 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after the current density was changed to 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> again. Most importantly, when the cycling stability was tested at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the OL-VMS-based anode also showed an excellent cycling capacity of 497.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a CE near 100%.</div><div class="NLM_p">As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>a, the cycling stabilities under different voltage ranges of 0.3–3, 0.2–3, and 0.01–3 V were also conducted at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. After 200 cycles, the stable reversible capacities of 511 and 487 mA g<sup>–1</sup> were achieved under the voltage conditions of 0.3–3 and 0.2–3 V, respectively. Interestingly, even though the voltage range was extended to 0.01–3 V, until 100 cycles, the obtained OL-VMS-based anode still delivered an outstanding specific capacity of 626.4 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. In contrast, under the same voltage condition as that on the OL-VMS-based anode, the cycling life of the anode based on VMS with the flower-like morphology prepared in the absence of NH<sub>4</sub>OH was tested at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. From <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>b, it was clearly seen that the reversible capacity decreased quickly from 642.4 to 331.7 mA g<sup>–1</sup> with an increase in the cycle, revealing that the anode with the orderly layered structure had better cycling stability when compared with that of the flower-like structure. Herein, the specific orderly layered structure could effectively mitigate the volume change during the process of discharging–charging. Most interestingly, even in the case without the conductive carbon additive (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>a), the OL-VMS-based anode with 2 mg cm<sup>–1</sup> loading amount also exhibited a remarkable specific capacity of 260 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after 130-cycle testing at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Such a remarkable specific capacity in the absence of conductive carbon could be owing to the improved metal-like conductivity caused by vanadium mediation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> In contrast, the stability of the electrodes with higher loading amounts of 1.8 and 4 mg cm<sup>–1</sup> was also measured. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>b, after 130 cycles, the OL-VMS-based anodes with 1.8 and 4 mg cm<sup>–1</sup> loading amounts exhibited 430.6 and 223.6 mA g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a> compares the electrochemical performance of the present OL-VMS-based anode with other VS<sub>2</sub>- and MoS<sub>2</sub>-based ones. Obviously, compared with the most reported VS<sub>2</sub>- and MoS<sub>2</sub>-based anodes (except VS<sub>2</sub> nanosheet and MoS<sub>2</sub>-G in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>), this OL-VMS-based anode exhibited a higher specific capacity. Thus, such an OL-VMS material should be a potential anode material candidate for SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">It has been proven that the charge storage mechanisms include the following two parts: one is the surface controlling process (pseudocapacitive), and the other is the diffusion-controlling process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45 ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 45 and 46">(45,46)</a> Thus, the following equation could be adopted to evaluate the mechanism of this material: <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47 ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 47 and 48">(47,48)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/medium/am1c05096_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i8"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span>where <i>v</i> and <i>i</i> are the scan rate and current, respectively, and <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are adjustable parameters. In general, the <i>b</i> value should be in the range of 0.5–1. The <i>b</i> value of 1 represents the surface-controlling process, whereas the <i>b</i> value approaching 0.5 represents the other charge storage mechanism. To further evaluate the mechanism of charge storage for the OL-VMS-based anode, the CV curves measured at 0.1, 0.3, 0.5, 0.7, 0.9, 1.2, and 1.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup> were collected (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a), and based on them, a linear correlation between the log <i>i</i> and log <i>v</i> for each peak was obtained (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b). According to the linear fitting, the <i>b</i> values of the peaks 1, 2, 3, and 4 were calculated to be 0.96, 0.95, 0.96, and 0.98, respectively. It should be noted that all of these <i>b</i> values were close to 1, revealing a dominant surface-controlling process by using such an OL-VMS-based anode, which contributed to the fast charge transport as well as an excellent rate performance. Furthermore, the total current was composed of two parts: one corresponded to the current of the capacitive process (surface-controlling process), and the other is ascribed to the current of the diffusion process, which could be calculated by the following equation: <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/medium/am1c05096_m004.gif" alt="" id="_i9"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> are adjustable parameters, <i>v</i> corresponds to the scan rate, and <i>i</i> is the value of current; <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> stand for the current values contributed by surface- and diffusion-controlling processes, respectively. According to the <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> values based on <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a>, the capacitive contributions at various scan rates can be known. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c shows the capacitive contribution of the OL-VMS-based anode at 1.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, in which a high contribution of 95.92% was achieved. Meanwhile, the contributions of the capacitive process at various scan rates were also known and are summarized in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d. The OL-VMS-based anode displayed a series of high capacitive contributions of 85.41, 87.76, 89.87, 91.45, 92.81, 94.44, and 95.92 corresponding to 0.1, 0.3, 0.5, 0.7, 0.9, 1.2, and 1.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, revealing an outstanding charge-transfer kinetics.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/medium/am1c05096_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/large/am1c05096_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) CV curves of the OL-VMS-based anode at different scan rates from 0.1 to 1.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Fitting line of log <i>i</i> vs log <i>v</i> plots for peaks 1–4. (c) Calculated capacitive contribution at 1.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (d) The content of capacitive contribution for OL-VMS at various scan rates from 0.1 to 1.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/large/am1c05096_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c05096&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a shows the Nyquist plots measured after 1, 5, 10, and 20 cycles at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> for the OL-VMS-based anode. Generally, the Nyquist plot consists of two sections: one is a semicircle in the high-frequency region, which is indexed to charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>); the other is a slope line in the low-frequency region assigned to the Warburg impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>) related to the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref50" aria-label="reference 14 and 50">(14,50)</a> According to the equivalent circuit, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values were calculated to be 19.12, 13.56, 10.52, and 11.85 Ω after 1, 5, 10, and 20 cycles, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>). Obviously, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value of the OL-VMS-based anode decreased gradually with an increase in the cycle, which could be attributed to the stable SEI layer formation and the enhanced electronic transfer kinetics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51 ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51 ref52" aria-label="reference 51 and 52">(51,52)</a> Furthermore, at the low-frequency region, the slope line related to the Warburg impedance was analyzed to evaluate the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> using the following two equations: <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref53" aria-label="reference 27 and 53">(27,53)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/medium/am1c05096_m005.gif" alt="" id="_i10"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq4"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/medium/am1c05096_m006.gif" alt="" id="_i11"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></div></div></span>where σ<sub>w</sub> is the Warburg impedance coefficient. According to the linear fitting between <i>Z</i>′ and ω<sup>–0.5</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b), the value of the Warburg impedance coefficient can be calculated based on the slope of the fitting line. Herein, the low Warburg impedance coefficient implies the high Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i>) according to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="eq 4">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="4">4</a>, indicating the fast diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup>. With an increase in cycle numbers, the Warburg coefficient gradually decreased from 53.95 to 31.43, revealing a fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, which could be attributed to the expansion of interlayer spacing during the charging–discharging process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a></div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/medium/am1c05096_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/large/am1c05096_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a) EIS Nyquist plots of the OL-VMS-based electrode after 1st, 5th, 10th, and 20th cycles (inset: equivalent circuit). (b) Linear fitting between <i>Z</i>′ and ω<sup>–1/2</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/large/am1c05096_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c05096&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To prove the structural stability, the SEM images of OL-VMS with an orderly layered structure and those of VMS with a flower-like structure after the cycling test were observed. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>, after one cycle (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>a,c), the structures of both materials were still maintained. However, after 10 cycles, the orderly layered structure of OL-VMS was still maintained (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>b), but the flower-like structure of the VMS was totally pulverized (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>d). Based on the above results, the OL-VMS with the orderly layered structure should be more difficult to pulverize during the ion intercalation/deintercalation process, as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>, resulting in an excellent cycling stability. In contrast, the VMS with the flower-like structure was more easily pulverized, leading to a short cycling life.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/medium/am1c05096_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/large/am1c05096_0009.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. Illustration of pulverization of different structures during the intercalation/deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/asset/images/large/am1c05096_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c05096&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In conclusion, a novel orderly layered VMoS<sub>2</sub> anode material was successfully obtained using a facile self-assembly hydrothermal method followed by a heating treatment process, which displayed an excellent electrochemical performance because of its specific nanostructure. It is found that more defects could be exposed at the edges of stacked nanosheets to offer abundant storage sites for Na<sup>+</sup>, which effectively enhanced the specific capacity. Meanwhile, the orderly stacked nanosheets with a vertical path and stable framework facilitated Na<sup>+</sup> transportation, which effectively promoted the rate performance and mitigated the volume expansion during the intercalation/deintercalation process, causing a good cycling stability. In consequence, the OL-VMS-based anode material exhibited a remarkable specific capacity (602.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>), an excellent cyclability (534 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 190 cycles at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>), and a superior rate capacity (452.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>) in the cutoff voltage range of 0.1–3 V. Moreover, the OL-VMS-based anode also showed an outstanding specific capacity of 626.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> even after 100 cycles at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. In addition, in the case without the conductive carbon addition, it still delivered good electrochemical properties with a high specific capacity (260 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 130 cycles in the 0.3–3 V voltage range). This work provides an effective strategy to simply synthesize layered materials for achieving high electrochemical performance, which could significantly improve the energy density and reduce the cost toward commercialization.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c05096?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c05096</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Characterizations of materials and electrochemical measurements; SEM images and XRD patterns of OL-VMS samples prepared under different conditions; cycling performance of the OL-VMS-based anode in different voltage ranges; cycling performance of the flower-like structure (prepared without NH<sub>4</sub>OH) in the voltage range of 0.01–3 V; cycling performance of OL-VMS without conductive carbon; comparison of electrochemical performance; and fitting results of Nyquist plots (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c05096/suppl_file/am1c05096_si_001.pdf">am1c05096_si_001.pdf (1.34 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.1c05096" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.  The battery systems reviewed here include sodium-sulfur batteries that are com. available for grid applications, redox-flow batteries that offer low cost, and lithium-ion batteries whose development for com. electronics and elec. vehicles is being applied to grid storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrZwDymuubZm7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhFk3jvyNYKIw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXhsVGktL%252FJ&amp;md5=5035bfda7631ad9d075e8836e61146ef</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201803609"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent Advances in Developing Hybrid Materials for Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lee, Jang Mee; Singh, Gurwinder; Cha, Wangsoo; Kim, Sungho; Yi, Jiabao; Hwang, Seong-Ju; Vinu, Ajayan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Energy Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1939-1966</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are an attractive prospect for energy storage in applications ranging from portable devices to large-scale energy storage grids.  However, practical application of SIBs is hindered by their large size and concomitant sluggish kinetics.  The hybridization strategy has been widely utilized as one of the effective ways to facilitate the enhancement of battery performance of the SIB electrodes.  A combination of transition metal compds. and carbonaceous species is a popular hybrid that could offer a wide range of possibilities for fabricating high-performance and long-lasting SIB anodes.  This Review specifically highlights hybridization strategies of transition metal compds., including metal oxides, metal sulfides, metal phosphides, and metal carbides with different carbon species.  Systematic discussion of the Na+-ion storage mechanism in these hybrids has been given special importance.  The effect of the hybridization on SIB performance is comprehensively covered in this Review to gauge the full impact of these materials as promising SIB anodic materials for the future.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqH0eRa5l__1rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhFk3jvyNYKIw"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">NASICON-type air-stable and all-climate cathode for sodium-ion batteries with low cost and high-power density</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen Mingzhe; Cortie David; Wang Enhui; Hu Zhe; Wang Xiaolin; Chou Shu-Lei; Dou Shi-Xue; Hua Weibo; Indris Sylvio; Xiao Jin; Xiao Jin; Chen Weihua; Wang Enhui; Gu Qinfen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">The development of low-cost and long-lasting all-climate cathode materials for the sodium ion battery has been one of the key issues for the success of large-scale energy storage.  One option is the utilization of earth-abundant elements such as iron.  Here, we synthesize a NASICON-type tuneable Na4Fe3(PO4)2(P2O7)/C nanocomposite which shows both excellent rate performance and outstanding cycling stability over more than 4400 cycles.  Its air stability and all-climate properties are investigated, and its potential as the sodium host in full cells has been studied.  A remarkably low volume change of 4.0% is observed.  Its high sodium diffusion coefficient has been measured and analysed via first-principles calculations, and its three-dimensional sodium ion diffusion pathways are identified.  Our results indicate that this low-cost and environmentally friendly Na4Fe3(PO4)2(P2O7)/C nanocomposite could be a competitive candidate material for sodium ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcTZ54fPflEXJ6x48Rj5GnGofW6udTcc2ebH1WH2pf-TWrntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BB3M%252FgtFKksw%253D%253D&amp;md5=4763fdc69b4373eda795ab200ba19ed6</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1002/anie.202008318"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Q.                 10.1002/anie.202008318</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D59%26publication_year%3D2020%26pages%3D17504-17510%26journal%3DAngew.%2BChem.%252C%2BInt.%2BEd.%26author%3DZ.%2BZhang%26author%3DY.%2BDu%26author%3DQ.%2BC.%2BWang%26author%3DJ.%2BXu%26author%3DY.%2BN.%2BZhou%26author%3DJ.%2BBao%26author%3DJ.%2BShen%26author%3DX.%2BZhou%26title%3DA%2BYolk-Shell-Structured%2BFePO4%2BCathode%2Bfor%2BHigh-Rate%2Band%2BLong-Cycling%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fanie.202008318&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.1c05096&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fanie.202008318&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Yolk-Shell-Structured FePO4 Cathode for High-Rate and Long-Cycling Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Zhuangzhuang; Du, Yichen; Wang, Qin-Chao; Xu, Jingyi; Zhou, Yong-Ning; Bao, Jianchun; Shen, Jian; Zhou, Xiaosi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">59</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">40</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">17504-17510</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACIEF5</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Amorphous iron phosphate (FePO4) has attracted enormous attention as a promising cathode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) because of its high theor. specific capacity and superior electrochem. reversibility.  Nevertheless, the low rate performance and rapid capacity decline seriously hamper its implementation in SIBs.  Herein, we demonstrate a sagacious multi-step templating approach to skillfully craft amorphous FePO4 yolk-shell nanospheres with mesoporous nanoyolks supported inside the robust porous outer nanoshells.  Their unique architecture and large surface area enable these amorphous FePO4 yolk-shell nanospheres to manifest remarkable sodium storage properties with high reversible capacity, outstanding rate performance, and ultralong cycle life.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq13OS6MCvasrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liW6wKtgE_vsA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhsFyhtrzF&amp;md5=cf3e684439234eac63ad3a0e9fd0bc00</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1016/j.jcis.2020.01.121"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Fabrication of Porous Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/Reduced Graphene Oxide Hollow Spheres with Enhanced Sodium Storage Performance</span>. <i>J. Colloid Interface Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>567</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">84</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">91</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.jcis.2020.01.121</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D567%26publication_year%3D2020%26pages%3D84-91%26journal%3DJ.%2BColloid%2BInterface%2BSci.%26author%3DJ.%2BXu%26author%3DE.%2BGu%26author%3DZ.%2BZhang%26author%3DZ.%2BXu%26author%3DY.%2BXu%26author%3DY.%2BDu%26author%3DX.%2BZhu%26author%3DX.%2BZhou%26title%3DFabrication%2Bof%2BPorous%2BNa3V2%2528PO4%25293%252FReduced%2BGraphene%2BOxide%2BHollow%2BSpheres%2Bwith%2BEnhanced%2BSodium%2BStorage%2BPerformance%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.jcis.2020.01.121&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.1c05096&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.jcis.2020.01.121&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fabrication of porous Na3V2(PO4)3/reduced graphene oxide hollow spheres with enhanced sodium storage performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Jingyi; Gu, Erlong; Zhang, Zhuangzhuang; Xu, Zhenhua; Xu, Yifan; Du, Yichen; Zhu, Xiaoshu; Zhou, Xiaosi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Colloid and Interface Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">567</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">84-91</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JCISA5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0021-9797</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have long been recognized as a potential substitute for lithium-ion batteries, while their practical application is greatly hindered owing to the absence of suitable cathode materials with improved rate capability and prolonged cycling life.  Na3V2(PO4)3 (NVP) has drawn extensive attention among the cathode materials for SIBs because of its fast Na+-transportable framework which enables high-speed charge transfer, but the poor elec. cond. of NVP significantly restricts the Na+ diffusion.  To tackle this issue, in this work, porous NVP/reduced graphene oxide hollow spheres (NVP/rGO HSs) are constructed via a spray drying strategy.  Due to the unique porous hollow architecture, the synthesized compd. manifests a high reversible capacity of 116 mAh g-1 at 1 C (1 C = 118 mA g-1), an outstanding high-rate capability of 107.5 mAh g-1 at 10 C and 98.5 mAh g-1 at 20 C, as well as a stable cycling performance of 109 mAh g-1 after 400 cycles at 1 C and 73.1 mAh g-1 after 1000 cycles at 10 C.  Moreover, galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique demonstrates that the Na+ diffusion coeff. of NVP/rGO HSs is an order of magnitude larger than the pristine NVP.  The remarkable electrochem. properties of NVP/rGO HSs in full cells further enable it a potential cathode for SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrleVP-NrHnSrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liW6wKtgE_vsA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXisVahsLY%253D&amp;md5=2ace87d94d1f8a8670c744bb5a3d5ce5</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms5033"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Matsuda, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ishii, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cumings, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Expanded Graphite as Superior Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nat.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphite, as the most common anode for com. Li-ion batteries, has been reported to have a very low capacity when used as a Na-ion battery anode.  It is well known that electrochem. insertion of Na+ into graphite is significantly hindered by the insufficient interlayer spacing.  Here we report expanded graphite as a Na-ion battery anode.  Prepd. through a process of oxidn. and partial redn. on graphite, expanded graphite has an enlarged interlayer lattice distance of 4.3 Å yet retains an analogous long-range-ordered layered structure to graphite.  In situ transmission electron microscopy has demonstrated that the Na-ion can be reversibly inserted into and extd. from expanded graphite.  Galvanostatic studies show that expanded graphite can deliver a high reversible capacity of 284 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 20 mA g-1, maintain a capacity of 184 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1, and retain 73.92% of its capacity after 2,000 cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrFEZ8wJYk1irVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liW6wKtgE_vsA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXksVeqs7g%253D&amp;md5=e4bc2edac8d22121e7986e165b7df007</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee40847g"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries have attracted great attention particularly in large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grid because of the huge abundant sodium resources and low cost.  In this article, a variety of electrode materials including cathodes and anodes as well as electrolytes for room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries are briefly reviewed.  We compare the difference in storage behavior between Na and Li in their analogous electrodes and summarize the sodium storage mechanisms in the available electrode materials.  This review also includes some new results from our group and our thoughts on developing new materials.  Some perspectives and directions on designing better materials for practical applications are pointed out based on knowledge from the literature and our experience.  Through this extensive literature review, the search for suitable electrode and electrolyte materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries is still challenging. In recent years, significant progress of Na ion battery anodes, which restricted the development of SIBs in the past decades, was achieved.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) with outstanding energy and power d. have been extensively investigated in recent years, rendering them the most suitable energy storage technol. for application in emerging markets such as elec. vehicles and stationary storage.  More recently, sodium, one of the most abundant elements on earth, exhibiting similar physicochem. properties as lithium, has been gaining increasing attention for the development of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) in order to address the concern about Li availability and cost-esp. with regard to stationary applications for which size and vol. of the battery are of less importance.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Heterostructure engineering is one of the most promising modification strategies toward improving sluggish kinetics for the anode of sodium ion batteries (SIBs).  Herein, we report a systemic investigation on the different types of heterostructure interfaces' effects of discharging products (Na2O, Na2S, Na2Se) on the rate performance.  First-principle calcns. reveal that the Na2S/Na2Se interface possesses the lowest diffusion energy barrier (0.39 eV) of Na among three kinds of interface structures (Na2O/Na2S, Na2O/Na2Se, and Na2S/Na2Se) due to its smallest recorded interface deformation, similar electronegativity, and lattice const.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Chronicle Review of Nonsilicon (Sn, Sb, Ge)-Based Lithium/Sodium-Ion Battery Alloying Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liang, Suzhe; Cheng, Ya-Jun; Zhu, Jin; Xia, Yonggao; Mueller-Buschbaum, Peter</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small Methods</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Since the commercialization of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in the early 1990s, tin (Sn), antimony (Sb), and germanium (Ge)-based anodes have attracted considerable research interest as promising candidates for next-generation LIBs due to their high theor. capacities, suitable operating voltages, and natural abundance.  Addnl., the awareness of limited global lithium sources promoted the renaissance of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) in recent years.  Sn, Sb, and Ge can electrochem. alloy with sodium and are regarded as promising anode candidates for high-performance SIBs.  However, these alloying/dealloying anodes suffer severe vol. expansion during lithiation or sodiation processes, which is one of the biggest obstacles toward practical applications.  In order to solve this problem, several strategies are developed including reducing the abs. size of particles, creating interior void space, and introducing buffer media.  After more than two decades efforts, the electrochem. performance of Sn, Sb, and Ge-based anodes is significantly improved.  Considerable studies about Sn, Sb, and Ge-based anodes are summarized in a chronicle perspective and the brief development histories.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrKTK1HNbFaELVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj53ZKWL178cw"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In situ simultaneous encapsulation of defective MoS2 nanolayers and sulfur nanodots into SPAN fibers for high rate sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Fenqiang; Feng, Xiaoshan; Zeng, Lingxing; Lin, Liangxu; Li, Xinye; Kang, Biyu; Xiao, Liren; Chen, Qinghua; Wei, Mingdeng; Qian, Qingrong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Molybdenum sulfide (MoS2) with layered structure has emerged as a promising anode material for sodium ion batteries (SIBs) in light of its particular surface chem. and phys. structures.  However, the MoS2-based SIBs usually suffered from the weaknesses of the low rate capability and poor cycling stability induced by the sluggish kinetics of Na+ intercalation and the diffluent discharge products.  Herein, the defective MoS2 nanocrystals and sulfur nanodots simultaneously embedded in sulfurized polyacrylonitrile (SPAN) fibers were fabricated via an electrospunning technol., followed by a simple annealing treatment.  The unique architecture, in which MoS2 nanolayers and sulfur nanodots were mounted inside the SPAN fiber, provided multi-entry and short-range channel for sodium ion to ensure a fast kinetics.  Besides, sulfur defects within MoS2 produced the strong chem. interaction for fixing sol. discharge products.  As a result, the electrode performed outstanding sodium-storage performance with a superior long cycling life (8000 cycles at 5 A g-1, 15,000 cycles at 10 A g-1) and excellent rate capability (212 mAh g-1 at 25 A g-1).
